<div id="review_384040091" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/384040091" />
    <a title="Mark" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9800512-mark"><img alt="Mark" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1339272218p2/9800512.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/384040091?book_show_action=true">Aug 04, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mark" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mark" href="/user/show/9800512-mark">Mark</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780312340704" href="/book/show/280366.Jar_City">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer384040091" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1582258359762554408">This is an intriguing novel, atmospherically and in its inexplicable ability to sustain the reader's interest -- despite the fact that practically everyone in it is strange, idiopathic, stilted or weird of articulation, practically autistically disconnected from reality, and committed to viewing everything generically and without depth or dimension, as though through the filter of a bad fifties cop drama. Dragnet, possibly, though without the cheeriness and empathy of Joe Friday.<br/><br/>Erlendur, the pr</span>
  <span id="freeText1582258359762554408" style="display:none">This is an intriguing novel, atmospherically and in its inexplicable ability to sustain the reader's interest -- despite the fact that practically everyone in it is strange, idiopathic, stilted or weird of articulation, practically autistically disconnected from reality, and committed to viewing everything generically and without depth or dimension, as though through the filter of a bad fifties cop drama. Dragnet, possibly, though without the cheeriness and empathy of Joe Friday.<br /><br />Erlendur, the protagonist, is a weary, bleak, generally enervated and ineffectual police inspector wIth the emotional IQ of a dysfunctional gibbon, who always, always says and does the wrong thing in any situation in which ordinary human sensitivity is demanded. He's not remotely stupid, and he solves a complex case that calls into question issues ranging from misogynistic practices in the treatment of rape cases to the questionable ethics of the virtual "jar cities" of genetic databases, but in so doing, he ignores the obvious, passively abuses practically everyone around him with an aggressive indifference, fails to prioritize his daughter's life or his own health -- or anything that matters, really -- over the dreary details of a disproportionately comprehensive investigation, and ends by precipitating the suicide of the hapless and essentially innocent perpetrator of the original crime. And yet the story somehow gains ever more traction, and Erlendur ever greater approximation of minimal human warmth, as matters progress from parental neglect to abuse of witnesses to desecration of a child's grave to the perfectly foreseeable fatal ineffectuality of Erlendur's final attempt to avert Einar's suicide. In the dénouement, Erlendur sits with the drug-addicted, pregnant daughter he'd earlier left to the streets, eating the meat stew she's prepared and inexplicably sensitively suggesting that she name her daughter after the lost child who's the central victim of the whole drama.<br /><br />Throughout, all the characters remark on how Icelandic everything is. Can you imagine saying not, "this is a typically clumsy murder," but "this is a typically clumsy, <i>American</i> murder?" Or it's just so <i>American,</i> the rainy weather today? Or I'd better take an <i>American</i> drive to the <i>American</i> supermarket? Is it a bad translation, or does the author think that everyone in Iceland is just unremittingly conscious of how Icelandic he or she is, and feels called upon constantly to remark on it? This sort of awkward, completely supererogatory, once-distanced observation goes on constantly, is committed by everybody, and gives rise to the sense that all the characters really do think they are cardboard cutouts in a minimalist screenplay by Lars Von Trier.<br /><br />And yet somehow it works, and it's even emotionally and intellectually satisfying. There's just no accounting for some Nordic novels.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1582258359762554408" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_168777082" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/168777082" />
    <a title="Carolyn" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5453616-carolyn"><img alt="Carolyn" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1311328196p2/5453616.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/168777082?book_show_action=true">May 16, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Carolyn" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Carolyn" href="/user/show/5453616-carolyn">Carolyn</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780312426385" href="/book/show/1139897.Jar_City">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5453616-carolyn?shelf=icelandic-fiction">icelandic-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5453616-carolyn?shelf=favourites">favourites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer168777082" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3068913206270326535">The detective Erlendur series is my favorite discovery of the past decade. There is absolutely nothing similar in the detective fiction genre, and this includes other Nordic crime fiction such as the Millenium trilogy. It is just so Icelandic. The main character is complex, flawed and a mass of contradictions. The author skilfully combines threads of different plots into a very effective narrative. <br/>Among the themes which are explored are guilt, loss of a child and coming to terms with the past (</span>
  <span id="freeText3068913206270326535" style="display:none">The detective Erlendur series is my favorite discovery of the past decade. There is absolutely nothing similar in the detective fiction genre, and this includes other Nordic crime fiction such as the Millenium trilogy. It is just so Icelandic. The main character is complex, flawed and a mass of contradictions. The author skilfully combines threads of different plots into a very effective narrative. <br />Among the themes which are explored are guilt, loss of a child and coming to terms with the past (or not). There are no cliched characters here: the victim elicits no sympathy at all, while we cannot fail to feel profound sympathy for the perpetrator.<br />Possibly one of the most fascinating aspects of the Erlendur series is the unfolding relationship between Erlendur and his drug-using daughter Eva. He is a man still traumatised by the disappearence of his brother in a blizzard when they were both children, and it has rendered him incapable of forming proper relationships ever since. <br />He seems to exhibit many of the symptoms of post traumatic stress disorder: the emotional numbness, guilt at having survived when his brother did not, the recurrent, obsessive recollections of the event, his problems with social and intimate relationships, and the general lack of joy in his life.<br /> Thus,in an act of self-absorbed callousness, he abandoned his wife and infant children when Eva was two, with disastrous consequences for Eva's development. The recurrent theme throughout the series is the missing person: Eva and Erlendur himself are both "missing persons" to themselves and eachother, and the reader feels that each one's salvation will be dependent on the finding of the other. They are so alike in their unhappiness, one taking drugs to ease her pain, and the other building a cold prison for himself to assuage his guilt.<br />Arnaldur writes for Icelanders, and doesn't compromise. There are no car chases, serial killers or other plot devices that are common to modern detective fiction, just excellent character development in an authentic Icelandic cultural and physical setting. <br />Jar City was the first of the series to be translated into English. Subsequent novels were Silence of the Grave, Voices, The Draining Lake, Arctic Chill and Hypothermia. There are three later ones awaiting translation, and they can't come soon enough for this reader! </span>
  <a data-text-id="3068913206270326535" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

Jar City is the third book in the series but the first to be translated into English. Although it is easy enough to catch on to the characters' back story, it was a bit slow going in the beginning as you sort through characters the author assumed you are already familiar with.   <span id="freeText4442579069745378805" style="display:none"><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/280366.Jar_City__Inspector_Erlendur__3_" title="Jar City (Inspector Erlendur #3) by Arnaldur Indriðason" rel="nofollow noopener">Jar City</a> is a Nordic noir mystery set in Reykjavik, Iceland where the population is so small they are known to one another primarily by their first names only. Jar City is the third book in the series but the first to be translated into English. Although it is easy enough to catch on to the characters' back story, it was a bit slow going in the beginning as you sort through characters the author assumed you are already familiar with. It is worth the sluggishness so keep reading. <br /><br />When an older man is murdered the suspect leaves behind a cryptic note, "I am HIM." The detectives discover that the victim had been accused of rape but never convicted. Could this be the meaning behind the note? Perhaps there are even more sinister connotations. Erlender, the protagonist, will have to find out. <br />From rape, depression, suicide, family dynamics, genetics and survivor's remorse - this book becomes far more than just another mystery and it left me eager to read the next in the series. </span>

  <span id="freeText3152550787233049335" style="display:none">This passed my <a href="http://readingenvy.blogspot.com/2015/12/reading-envy-047-sex-with-elvis-book.html" rel="nofollow noopener">book speed-dating test</a> from back in September, but in the end this was not a book I needed to finish. <br /><br />Part of the problem is that the publisher translating these started with this book, but this one is #3. I kept feeling there were details I was supposed to already know about the Inspector that would better illuminate some of the events, most notably the relationships with the other detective and his daughter. I'm not sure why you would start with book #3, but I really only picked this up at a used book store anyway. <br /><br />There are some curious bits that are pure Iceland, which is the reason I brought the book home - mysterious sewage issues and landscape and strange justice practices - but the central crime and mystery itself seemed really uninteresting, without a lot of pieces to discover. </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_550133964" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/550133964" />
    <a title="Harry" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7597429-harry"><img alt="Harry" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1380595404p2/7597429.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/550133964?book_show_action=true">Mar 02, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Harry" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Harry" href="/user/show/7597429-harry">Harry</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780312340704" href="/book/show/280366.Jar_City">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7597429-harry?shelf=nordic-crime-fiction">nordic-crime-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7597429-harry?shelf=detective-mystery">detective-mystery</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer550133964" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1309074630850906096">Arnaldur Indridason was born in Reykjavík on 28 January 1961, the son of writer Indriði G. Þorsteinsson. Arnaldur is considered one of the most popular writers in Iceland in recent years — topping bestseller lists time and again. In 2004, his books were 7 of the 10 most popular titles borrowed in Reykjavík City Library. His book <i>Jar City</i> was turned into a film with the same title. (no, not available on Netflix for streaming, which sucks!)<br/><br/>If you've read any of my <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/546324612' rel='nofollow noopener'>Nesbo reviews</a> than you know all a</span>
  <span id="freeText1309074630850906096" style="display:none">Arnaldur Indridason was born in Reykjavík on 28 January 1961, the son of writer Indriði G. Þorsteinsson. Arnaldur is considered one of the most popular writers in Iceland in recent years — topping bestseller lists time and again. In 2004, his books were 7 of the 10 most popular titles borrowed in Reykjavík City Library. His book <i>Jar City</i> was turned into a film with the same title. (no, not available on Netflix for streaming, which sucks!)<br /><br />If you've read any of my <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/546324612" rel="nofollow noopener">Nesbo reviews</a> than you know all about ennui, a bleak and deterministic view on life. My goodness, Nordic thrillers are conspicuous for not containing even a smidgeon of humor. I mean, Elvis Cole's jolliness would shrivel and freeze instantly if setting foot in Iceland...you know, right? (Pike would probably enjoy it.) Ok. Ok. Arnaldur describes it like this:<br /><br /><i>“The other option you have is to come to terms with this fucking life, as you call it, and put up with the suffering it involves. Put up with the suffering we all have to endure, always, to get through that and find and enjoy the happiness and joy that it brings us as well, in spite of our being alive.”</i> <br /><br />But, get this! Guys like Arnaldur, Henning, Jo, and Lars are getting translated, their books shipped all over the world, movies are made and yes, the latest barges are hitting our mother homeland: the US. You know, right? That's just what we need, a blast of cold, freezing air to hit our shores. And it doesn't stop there. Maybe we need something to temper our humor, our laissez-faire approach to just about everything. Cause here it is: America is loving these Nordic thrillers! <br /><br />The Erlender series is a very good example of what's hitting our shores (kinhda like the Greek Yogurt appearing everywhere in grocery stores, seemingly out of the blue). Personally, I think <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/904719.Jo_Nesb_" rel="nofollow noopener">Jo Nesbo</a> is better at it...but if tempting to set this on equal footing, perhaps I'd place Erlender side by side with <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/search?utf8=%E2%9C%93&amp;query=wallander" rel="nofollow noopener">Wallander</a>. What seems to be prevalent among all Nordic writers is that their heroes are better at grasping the seemingly insignificant and pursuing that insignificance in defiance of all obstacles to solve crimes. Erlender, for example, spots an obscure and old photograph in a victim's apartment and instead of pursuing canned motives for murder, instead stubbornly pursues the history behind the photograph even though it is decades old and doesn't appear to have anything to do with the current crime and he does so in defiance of his superiors. American authors just don't do that. With them, it's either money, passion, revenge or plain madness (serial killers). All motives for murderous crimes belong in one category or another...and these categories are the starting point for American detective stories. Perhaps our heroes are not contextually mature enough, I don't know. But I know this is what appeals to me in Nordic novels. Let's just say I better understand the motives of Nordic villains as a result (maybe even emphatize with the motive). <br /><br />And yes, I have a heater on besides my reading chair with a cup of hot chocolate, just in case I start to feel cold, damp, and depressed while reading this stuff!). You gotta love it...:)<br /><br />Ok, as with all series reviews. This one covers all the Erlender series. If you've read this one Erlender review, you've read 'em all. Enjoy!</span>
  <a data-text-id="1309074630850906096" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('6H6rc84+HRenAqDyVyBAghcSauGux4DNuVV0WlGkEoUXeHNcLQpqvWSdxQ5riArFeNh5BOPfecf/Azz54CVZ1Q==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('Wjo6WmWhb0R9R1lTq359vEFf02UHT3BgKGeTcBGKQU2lPOJ1hpUY7r7YPK+X1jf7LpXAgEpXiWpuMdvToAsKHQ==')})
  }

  <span id="freeText6900787749668058668" style="display:none">Arnaldur Indriðason has the rare distinction of having won the Nordic Crime Novel Prize two years running. He is also the winner of the highly respected and world famous CWA Gold Dagger Award for the top crime novel of the year in the English language, Silence of the Grave. <br /><br />Arnaldur’s novels have sold over 14 million copies worldwide, in 40 languages, and have won numerous well-respected prizes and received rave reviews all over the world.<br /><br /></span>

//<![CDATA[
      var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_1905888'), "\n\n  <h2><a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1905888.Menschens_hne?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false\">Menschensöhne (Kommissar Erlendur, #1)<\/a><\/h2>\n\n      <div>\n        by <a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/6861265.Arnaldur_Indri_ason?origin=series_works\">Arnaldur Indriðason<\/a>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p6\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p0\"><\/span><\/span> 3.56 avg rating &mdash; 1,757 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 1997\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer2747917486771831998\">Zwei kurz aufeinander folgende Todesfälle beschäftigen die Mordkommission Reykjaviks unter Kommissar Erlendur Sveinsson: Der pensionierte Lehrer Halldór Svavarsson stirbt in den Flammen, die sein kleines Holzhaus vernichtet haben. Dabei deuten alle S<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText2747917486771831998\" style=\"display:none\">Zwei kurz aufeinander folgende Todesfälle beschäftigen die Mordkommission Reykjaviks unter Kommissar Erlendur Sveinsson: Der pensionierte Lehrer Halldór Svavarsson stirbt in den Flammen, die sein kleines Holzhaus vernichtet haben. Dabei deuten alle Spuren darauf hin, dass er Opfer eines brutalen Mordes wurde. Etwa zur gleichen Zeit begeht der Patient einer Nervenklinik Selbstmord: Daníel, der sich seit mehr als fünfundzwanzig Jahren in psychiatrischer Behandlung befand, war ein ehemaliger Schüler Svavarssons.  In Menschensöhne ermittelt der isländische Kommissar Erlendur Sveinsson in seinem ersten Fall, der weit in die Vergangenheit zurückreicht. Wie beide Todesfälle zusammenhängen und wie es zu erklären ist, dass fast alle Schüler einer früheren Klasse Svavarssons unter mysteriösen Umständen ums Leben gekommen sind, darauf stößt der Polizist erst durch die Hilfe von Pálmi, dem Bruder von Daníel. Doch Pálmi sucht gleichzeitig auf eigene Faust nach den Hintergründen und Hintermännern der mysteriösen Geschichte und begibt sich damit in große Gefahr ...  Eines steht nach dem insgesamt fünften von Arnaldur Indridason auf Deutsch erschienenen Kriminalromanen fest: Das ist Pflichtlektüre für alle Island-Reisende! Erzählerisch eindrucksvoll dringt der Autor tief in die Zerrissenheit des kleinen isländischen Inselvolks ein. Indridasons Geschichten beschreiben zerstörte Existenzen und ihre Verzweiflungstaten. Und fast immer erzählt er gleichzeitig Familiengeschichten, die meist tragisch enden oder die eine dunkle Vergangenheit verborgen halten. Selbst sein Kommissar Sveinsson leidet unter seiner Beziehungslosigkeit und einer gescheiterten Ehe, von der ihm nur eine drogensüchtige und rebellische Tochter geblieben ist.  Arnaldur Indridason stößt wie einst Georges Simenon in seinen Krimis zu einem existenziellen Kern vor. Gute Kriminalromane zeichnen sich vor allem dadurch aus, wie sie Menschen in Grenzsituationen darstellen. Nun erscheinen Indridassons Darstellungen des Lebens auf Island schon selbst als Grenzsituation. Doch Menschensöhne ist überdies eine Geschichte von der Arroganz und Skrupellosigkeit der Mächtigen, die schonungslos dargestellt und in ihrer Unmenschlichkeit entlarvt wird. Indridason zaubert vor diesem Hintergrund zwar letzten Endes eine teilweise unglaubwürdige Geschichte aus dem Hut, dennoch schaudert einem vor dem Zynismus und der Unbelehrbarkeit der Herrschenden, die er mit seiner Parabel entlarvt. -- Christian Koch<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"2747917486771831998\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; return false;\">...more<\/a>\n\n    <\/div>\n\n
